بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم وبالله العون والعصمة والتوفيق. الحمد لواهب العقل حمدًا يليق بغرائب أصناف صنائعه وعجائب أنواع بدائعه من العقول والحواس والنفوس الحساسة من الفصول والأجناس أبدعها بقدرته الشايعة، وأودع في طباعها لحكمته البالغة من الخصائص النفسانية نحو الحدوس في النفوس الإنسانية لتحصل بوسائطها الأصول من القواعد الحكمية، ويمكن بطرائقها الوصول إلى العقائد الحكمية التي هي ذريعة إلى المقاصد الدينية إنه العلم الإلهي، والمطالب اليقينية جلَّت صنائعه من أن يحيط بها كياسة الناس بالأمثال والنظير بحمده ، ولا حمد إلا دون نعماته، ونتوكل عليه بعد حمده وثنائه، ونسأله أن يصلي على محمد خاتم أنبيائه وعلى آله وأصحابه وأوليائه. وبعد: فإن العلم بحقائق الأشياء أشرف الفضائل وأولاها وأعظمها وأعلاها، وكيف لا ؟! والمعارف القدسية الإلهية، مسائل العلم الإلهية تعرف به وتؤلف منه، فلا شرف إلا وهو السبيل إليه، ولا لطف إلا وهو الدليل عليه، منه تتضح أسباب السعادات وتنتج أنوار الكرامات؛ لا سيما العلم الذي هو أصل العلوم وأساسها ومقطعها ومقياسها وهو علم المنطق، لولاه لتحيرت العقول في الأحكام، واضطرت الشبه الفحول في التفهم والإفهام؛ لما أن الأحكام في العلوم مجملة لولا التعرض لمعانيه؛ والبراهين فيها مهملة بدون النظر في مبانيه، فالتحرير في المباحثة لا يكون إلا ببيانه، والتقرير في المناظرة لا يمكن
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And with Allah is the help, protection, and success. Praise be to the Bestower of intellect, praise befitting the wondrous varieties of His creations and the marvels of types of His arts, from intellects, senses, and sensitive souls from the species and genera. He created them with His pervasive power, and deposited in their natures, for His far-reaching wisdom, from the psychological properties such as intuition in human souls, so that through their means the principles from wisdom-based rules may be obtained, and by their methods access may be possible to wisdom-based beliefs which are a means to religious objectives. Indeed it is the divine science and certain demands. Exalted are His creations beyond what the sagacity of people can encompass by way of parables and likenesses in His praise. There is no praise except inadequate for His blessings. And we rely on Him after praising and extolling Him, and we ask Him to bless Muhammad, the seal of His prophets, and his family, companions, and friends. And thereafter: Indeed knowledge of the realities of things is the most noble of virtues and the foremost and greatest and highest. How could it not be? Divine holy knowledges, the matters of divine science, are known through it and composed from it. So there is no nobility except that it is the path to it, and no subtlety except that it is the guide to it. From it the causes of felicities become clear and the lights of miracles are produced. Especially the science that is the root of sciences and their foundation, their divider and their measure, which is the science of logic. If not for it, intellects would be confused in judgments, and strong doubts would force (people) to struggle in understanding and making understood. This is because judgments in sciences are vague without engaging their meanings, and proofs in them are neglected without examining their structures. Therefore precise investigation in inquiry cannot be achieved except by explaining it, and proper formulation in debate cannot be...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is the opening (front matter) of the book. The author begins with the basmalah, then praises Allah as the Bestower of intellect and all faculties. He highlights that Allah created intellects, senses, and souls with psychological properties like intuition, so that through them humans can attain wisdom-based rules and ultimately religious objectives. The author then states that knowledge of realities is the most noble virtue, especially divine knowledge. He emphasizes that logic is the root, foundation, divider, and measure of all sciences. Without logic, intellects would be confused in judgments and people would struggle to understand and communicate, because judgments in sciences are vague without their meanings, and proofs are neglected without examining their structures. Therefore precise investigation and debate depend on logic. The passage serves to establish the indispensable role of logic in achieving certainty in religious and philosophical matters.

Key Takeaway
Logic is not optional—it is the root, foundation, divider, and measure of all sciences, and without it, intellects are confused and proofs remain neglected.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Science of Logic: Root, Foundation, and Measure of All Sciences
Author praises logic and explains his motivation for writing the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three key roles the author assigns to logic and explain why he considers it essential for all sciences.

Core Idea
Logic is the root, foundation, separator, and measure of all sciences.

The author opens his work with a powerful praise of logic, placing it at the center of all intellectual disciplines. He argues that without logic, intellects would be perplexed and proofs would remain hidden. This lesson will help you unpack his arguments and understand why he considers logic indispensable for Islamic sciences and religious objectives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's description of logic
Read the Arabic passage and locate the exact phrases where the author defines logic. He uses four key terms: 'root' (أصل), 'foundation' (أساس), 'separator' (مقطع), and 'measure' (مقياس). Note them down.
These four terms emphasize logic's foundational and evaluative role.
2
Analyze the consequences of lacking logic
The author states: 'Without it, intellects would be perplexed in judgments, and proofs would be neglected.' Reflect on why he says this. What would go wrong in the study of any science if logic were absent?
3
Connect logic to religious objectives
The author mentions that logic helps attain 'philosophical beliefs' which are a means to 'religious objectives' (المقاصد الدينية). Trace this link: how does logical reasoning support the goals of Islam?
This shows the integration of logic into the Islamic intellectual tradition.
4
Evaluate the author's motivation for writing
The author responds to a request from the elite for a work that combines concise rules with necessary additions. Identify his critique of existing books: some are too wordy, others too brief. What does he promise in his own work?
This sets the stage for the content of the book.

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And with Allah is assistance, protection, and success. Praise be to the Bestower of intellect, a praise befitting the wondrous types of His handiworks and the marvelous kinds of His creations from intellects, senses, and sensitive souls from the species and genera. He originated them with His pervasive power, and deposited in their natures, by His perfect wisdom, of the psychical properties such as intuition in human souls, so that through their means the principles from the philosophical foundations may be obtained, and through their methods access may be had to the philosophical beliefs which are a means to religious objectives—indeed it is the divine science—and the certain demands. Glorious are His handiworks beyond what human acumen can encompass by similitudes and likenesses in His praise, and there is no praise except that it falls short of His blessings. And we rely upon Him after His praise and extolment, and we ask Him to bless Muhammad, the seal of His prophets, and his family, companions, and friends. And after: indeed knowledge of the realities of things is the most noble of virtues and the first and greatest and highest of them. How could it not be? The divine holy knowledges, the issues of the divine science, are known through it and composed from it. So there is no nobility except that it is the path to it, and no subtlety except that it is the guide to it. From it are clarified the causes of happiness and produced the lights of noble miracles. Especially the science that is the root of sciences, their foundation, their separator, and their measure—the science of logic. Without it, intellects would be perplexed in judgments, and great doubts would force the understanding and comprehension. Because judgments in sciences are summary without addressing their meanings, and proofs in them are neglected without scrutiny of their structures. So precision in research cannot be attained except through its clarification, and verification in disputation cannot be achieved except by mastering it. And what is such is among the things that souls are infatuated with, and aspirations are directed to, due to the need of all other sciences for encompassing it and mediation from it. So the elite of the nation concern themselves with the measures and attend to the conditions of request time after time to obtain from the methods what is beneficial and from the marvels what is excellent. So after observing these conditions and the conditions of this observation, they requested from me a copy containing the rules in their entirety and the necessary additions likewise. For if it includes rare benefits and marvelous advantages, the benefit would be more complete and the return more abundant and general. And the copies in this art are numerous, both concise and expanded. The expanded ones are only expanded in terms of words that have no substance, and the concise ones are only concise in terms of meanings that are indispensable at the start, and it is not doubted that they are insufficient for the purposes, neither these nor those. And the intended subtleties were intelligible in both, so I responded to their request due to their sincerity in seeking, and I undertook that, for they are among the clever people. And within this undertaking was that which includes the attainment of the desired goal. That is among the things whose neglect would be contrary to reason. And it has been said concerning what is intended in the faults of people, something like the deficiency of those able to perfect. So I left that leaving by undertaking it, relying on the Pre-Eternal Presence, for He is over what He wills Powerful and worthy of answering.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to the book. It begins with the basmalah and praise of Allah as the Bestower of intellect, highlighting the divine origin of cognitive faculties. The author then declares that knowledge of realities is the noblest virtue, and the science of logic is the root, foundation, separator, and measure of all sciences. He argues that without logic, intellects would be confused and proofs neglected. The author responds to a request from the 'elite of the nation' for a comprehensive work that combines both concise rules and necessary additions, noting that existing works are either overly verbose or insufficient. He commits to writing this book, relying on Allah.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Why Logic is Indispensable for All Sciences
Author outlines the importance of logic and explains his motivation to write a comprehensive text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's argument for why logic is a necessary tool for mastering other sciences, and explain why he found existing logical texts insufficient.

Core Idea
Logic is the tool that perfects every other science.

In this opening passage, the author explains why logic (al-mantiq) is not just another discipline, but the foundational instrument that all other sciences depend upon. He describes how the intellectual elite eagerly seek mastery of syllogisms and the conditions of sound reasoning. The author then reveals his motivation for writing this book: existing works are either too wordy or too concise, so he aims to produce a balanced text that includes all the essential rules and valuable insights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's central claim about logic
Read the first few sentences: logic is described as a tool ('ala) that other sciences need. The author says other sciences cannot be perfected 'except by mastering it.' This establishes logic's foundational role.
2
Recognize who the 'elite' are and their focus
The author mentions 'the elite of the nation' (safwat al-umma) who are concerned with syllogisms (al-aqyis) and the conditions of sound reasoning. This shows that logical training is particularly important for scholars engaged in deep intellectual work.
3
Understand the request made to the author
The elite requested from the author a comprehensive work that includes 'the rules in their entirety' and 'necessary additions.' This indicates that they found existing texts inadequate.
4
Analyze the author's critique of existing works
The author divides existing logical texts into two types: expanded ones with useless verbosity (mabsuta) and precise ones that are too brief (mudabita). Both fail to satisfy the needs of students, so the author decides to write a new book that combines rare benefits and wonderful benefits.
5
Examine the author's motivation and resolution
He states that abandoning this task would be 'contrary to reason,' so he proceeds with reliance on God. This shows his conviction that producing a proper logic text is an intellectual duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطق الكبير
إلا بإتقانه. وما يكون كذلك فذلك من جملة ما تكون النفوس مشغوفة به، والهمم مصروفة إليه لافتقار ما عداه من العلوم إلى الإحاطة به والوساطة منه. فالصفوة من الأمة يهتمون إذن للأقياس، ويتعرضون لشرائط الالتماس طورًا بعد طور ليحصل لهم من الأساليب ما يفيد ومن الأعاجيب ما يُجيد؛ فيلتمسون بعد رعاية هذه الشرائط وشرائط هذه الرعاية مني نسخة مشتملة على القواعد جملتها وعلى الزوائد اللازمة معرفتها كذلك. فإنها إذا كانت متضمنة للفرائد الغريبة والفوائد العجيبة كانت الإفادة أتم والإعادة أكثر وأعم، والنسخ في هذا الفن كثيرة، مضبوطة ومبسوطة؛ فالمبسوطة منها لا تكون إلا وأن يكون بسطها بحسب الألفاظ التي لا طائل تحتها والمضبوطة أيضًا لا تكون إلا وأن [۲] يكون ضبطها بحسب المعاني التي لا بد منها في الابتداء ولا يستراب في أنها لا تكون كافية في المقاصد لا هذه ولا تلك، وقد كانت اللطائف المقصودة معقولة فيهما فنلتفت التماسهم لصدقهم في الالتماس والتزمت ذلك فإنهم بين الأنام من الأكياس. وقد كان في ضمن هذا الالتزام ما يشتمل على تحصيل المرام؛ وذلك إذن من جملة ما يكون تركه على خلاف العقل. وقد قيل لما يراد في عيوب الناس شيئًا كنقص القادرين على التمام فتركتُ ذلك الترك بالإقدام عليه متوكلا على الحضرة الأزلية، فإنه على ما يشاء قدير وبالإجابة جدير.
The Great Logic. Except by mastering it. And what is like that is among the things that souls are passionate about, and ambitions are directed towards it, due to the need of other sciences to encompass it and mediate from it. So the elite of the nation pay attention to syllogisms, and expose themselves to the conditions of seeking after stage after stage, so that they may obtain from methods what is beneficial, and from wonders what is excellent. So they seek, after observing these conditions and the conditions of this observation, from me a copy that includes the rules in their entirety and the necessary additions that must be known. For if it contains rare gems and wonderful benefits, the benefit would be complete and the repetition more and more general. The copies in this art are many, precise and expanded. The expanded ones are only with expansion according to words that have no benefit underneath, and the precise ones are only with precision according to the meanings that are indispensable in the beginning, and there is no doubt that they are not sufficient for the purposes, neither these nor those. And the intended subtleties were understood in both, so I responded to their request due to their truthfulness in asking, and I undertook that, for they are among the intelligent people. And within this undertaking was what includes the attainment of the goal; so that is among the things whose abandonment is contrary to reason. And it was said: 'Whatever is desired in the faults of people is something like the deficiency of those able to complete.' So I abandoned that abandonment by proceeding with it, relying on the Eternal Presence, for He is powerful over what He wills and worthy of answering. [Section on Wisdom]
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a front matter introduction where the author establishes the importance of logic. He begins by stating that no science can be perfected except by mastering logic (mantiq). Logic is the tool (ala) that other sciences depend on; it is the mediator that provides the method for sound reasoning. The author then describes how the elite (al-safwa) of the Muslim community are deeply concerned with logical reasoning (al-aqyis, i.e., syllogisms) and seek the proper conditions for sound investigation. They requested from the author a comprehensive text that includes all the foundational rules (al-qawa'id) and necessary additions (al-zawa'id). The author notes that existing works are either too verbose (mabsuta) with empty words or too brief (mudabita) with only essential meanings, neither fully satisfying. He therefore undertakes to write a work that combines rare benefits and wonderful insights, fulfilling their request. He concludes by emphasizing that abandoning this task would be irrational, and he relies on the divine presence for success.

المنطق
الأقياس
القواعد

Key Takeaway
Logic is the indispensable mediating tool that all sciences require for sound reasoning, and the author writes this book to fill the gap left by existing insufficient texts.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Logic as Instrumental Science vs. Sciences Sought for Themselves
Author divides sciences into instrumental (logic) and sought-for-themselves (theoretical/practical).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sciences sought as means (instrumental) and sciences sought for themselves, and identify logic as an instrumental science.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge is sought for its own sake; some knowledge is a tool for attaining other sciences.

The passage opens by stating the purpose of wisdom: to know realities for happiness. It then divides sciences into those sought as aids (like logic) and those sought for themselves. The latter are then split into theoretical and practical. This lesson focuses on the first division: means vs. ends.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of wisdom
The author states that the purpose of wisdom (ḥikma) is to arrive at the realities of things to attain happiness. This sets the teleological framework for the classification.
2
Find the first division of sciences
The passage says that sciences are either sought not for themselves but as aids to other sciences (logic) or sought for themselves. Underline that logic is the instrumental science.
3
Understand the second division: theoretical vs. practical
Those sought for themselves are either theoretical (depend on what is not our choice) or practical (depend on our choice). Note the criterion of human choice.
Theoretical sciences aim at belief alone; practical at action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستراب في أن الغرض من الحكمة أن يوقف على حقائق الأشياء بحسب ما يمكنه الوقوف عليه. فإن الاستعداد للسعادة كما هو حقه لا يحصل إلا بالاطلاع على الحقائق وهو الاطلاع على العلوم الحكمية بالحقيقة. وما يكون منها فذلك لا يمكن أن يتغير بتغير الأزمان ولا أن يتبدل بتبدل الأديان كذلك بل يكون كما كان من الأزل إلى الأبد. فإن علمنا بأن الكل أعظم من جزئه مثلا، لا يمكن أن يكون في الأزل على خلاف ما يكون في الأبد على أنه من حيث هو هو ينقسم إلى أقسام مختلفة، فإن منها ما يكون مطلوبًا لا لذاته بل للغير وذلك أن يكون معونة لتحصيل ما عداه من العلوم وهو المنطق، ومنها ما يكون مطلوبًا لذاته.
وذلك إما أن يكون علمًا بما لا يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم النظرية، وإما أن يكون علما بما يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم العملية؛ والعلوم العملية قد تسمى فلسفة عمليةً، والمقصود من النظرية اعتقاد رأي فقط، ومن العملية اعتقاد رأي لأجل العمل، فالنظرية أولى بأن تُنسب إلى الرأي وما يكون فيها من الموجودات.
فذلك قد يكون من جملة ما يخالط الحركة نحو الإنسانية مثلا، وقد يكون من جملة ما لا يخالط نحو الملكية. والأول إما أن لا يمكن تجرده عن مادة معينة لا في القوام ولا في الوهم، وإما أن يمكن وذلك في الوهم لا في القوام. وبالجملة فالعلوم النظرية تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام؛ لأنه [أ] إما أن يكون وجوده متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج والذهن جميعا وهو العلم الطبيعي، [ب] أو متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج لا في الذهن وهو الرياضي [ت] أو لا يكون متعلقا بالمادة لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن وهو الإلهي؛ ولا مجال للقسم الرابع فإن من المحال أن يكون متعلقا بالمادة في الذهن لا في الخارج.
ثم كل واحد من الطبيعي والرياضي والإلهي فإنه قد يقوم مقام الأصل؛ وذلك على أقسام، وقد يقوم مقام الفرع؛ وذلك على أقسام كذلك يعرف من الكتب المخصوصة ببيانها.
وأما العلوم العملية فإنها تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام أيضًا؛ وذلك لأن ما يكون منها فذلك [أ] إما أن يكون متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المخصوصة بالإنسان وحده من الفضائل والرذائل؛ وهو علم الأخلاق [ب] أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين خواصه؛ وهو علم تدبير المنزل، [ت] أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين عامة الناس؛ وهو علم تدبير المدينة فهذه هي العلوم السياسية: الأول للنفس، والثاني لأهل المنزل، والثالث لأهل المدينة؛ على أن المشهور من علم السياسة والمفهوم منه هو الثالث منها.
ثم الغاية في الحكمة النظرية كما مرَّ ذكرها من قبل - معرفة الحق، وفي العملية معرفة الخير. ومن يؤتى الاستكمال بها من الحكمتين فقد أوتي خيرًا كثيرًا؛ وَذَلِكَ فَضْلُ اللَّهِ يُؤْتِيهِ مَن يَشَاءُ وَاللَّهُ ذُو الْفَضْلِ الْعَظِيمِ.
There is no doubt that the purpose of wisdom is to arrive at the realities of things as far as possible. For readiness for happiness, as is its due, is only attained by insight into realities, which is insight into the philosophical sciences in truth. And what pertains to them cannot change with changing times nor alter with changing religions, but remains as it was from eternity to eternity. For our knowledge that the whole is greater than its part, for example, cannot be in eternity contrary to what it is in eternity. Since it in itself divides into different divisions, among them is that which is sought not for itself but for something else, being an aid to acquiring other sciences—that is logic—and among them is that which is sought for itself. And that is either knowledge of what does not exist by our choice, which is theoretical science, or knowledge of what exists by our choice, which is practical science. Practical sciences may be called practical philosophy. The aim of theoretical is mere belief in an opinion, and of practical is belief in an opinion for the sake of action. Thus theoretical is more properly attributed to opinion and to what exists among existents. That may be part of what is mixed with motion, like humanity, for example, or part of what is not mixed, like divinity. The first is either incapable of being abstracted from a specific matter both in substance and in estimation, or capable of abstraction in estimation but not in substance. In summary, theoretical sciences divide into three divisions: [A] either its existence is attached to matter both externally and mentally, which is natural science; [B] or attached to matter externally but not mentally, which is mathematical; [T] or not attached to matter either externally or mentally, which is divine. There is no fourth division, for it is impossible that it be attached to matter mentally but not externally. Then each of natural, mathematical, and divine may serve as the original, which has its own divisions, or as the derivative, which likewise has its own divisions, known from the books specifically devoted to their explanation. As for practical sciences, they also divide into three divisions: [A] either attached to opinions that regulate the states specific to the individual alone, of virtues and vices—that is ethics; [B] or attached to opinions that regulate the states common between him and his household—that is household management; [T] or attached to opinions that regulate the states common between him and the general public—that is political science. These are the political sciences: the first for the soul, the second for the household, the third for the city, though the commonly known political science is the third. Then the goal of theoretical wisdom, as mentioned before, is knowledge of truth, and of practical wisdom is knowledge of good. Whoever attains perfection through these two wisdoms has attained abundant good. And that is the bounty of Allah which He gives to whom He wills, and Allah is the possessor of great bounty.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of sciences. It begins by stating the purpose of wisdom: to know realities as far as possible, which is necessary for happiness. The author then divides sciences into two categories: those sought as means (instrumental) and those sought for themselves. Logic is instrumental. The sought-for-themselves sciences are further divided into theoretical and practical based on whether their subject matter depends on our choice. Theoretical sciences aim at belief alone; practical sciences aim at action. The theoretical sciences are subdivided into three: natural science (deals with material things that cannot be abstracted from matter), mathematical science (deals with material things that can be abstracted mentally), and divine science (deals with immaterial things). The author asserts there is no fourth type. Then he notes that each of these can be either original or derivative. Practical sciences are also three: ethics (individual character), household management (family affairs), and politics (city affairs). The passage ends by stating the ultimate aims: truth for theoretical wisdom, good for practical wisdom, and praises Allah. In Islamic tradition, these correspond to known disciplines: natural science studies physical bodies (e.g., plants, animals), mathematics studies numbers and shapes, divine science studies God and angels. Practical: ethics deals with virtues like justice, household management with family relations, politics with just governance.

العلوم العملية
العلم الطبيعي
العلم الرياضي
العلم الإلهي
علم الأخلاق
تدبير المنزل
تدبير المدينة

C) It is an instrumental science sought as an aid to other sciences.
D) It is a branch of divine science.

Key Takeaway
Logic is an instrumental science, not an end in itself; it serves the acquisition of theoretical and practical sciences.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Three Theoretical Sciences: Natural, Mathematical, Divine
Author subdivides theoretical sciences into natural, mathematical, and divine based on matter attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify natural science, mathematics, and divine science based on their attachment to matter, and explain the impossibility of a fourth type.

Core Idea
Theoretical sciences are classified by the extent to which their subject matter depends on matter.

After dividing sciences into theoretical and practical, the author subdivides theoretical sciences into three based on their relationship to matter: fully material (natural), abstractable (mathematical), and immaterial (divine). This distinction is foundational for understanding the hierarchy of knowledge in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the threefold division
The passage says theoretical sciences divide into three based on attachment to matter. Find the Arabic letters أ, ب, ت marking the three divisions.
Read carefully: 'إما أن يكون وجوده متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج والذهن جميعا' etc.
2
Understand the first: natural science
Natural science deals with objects that cannot be abstracted from matter even in the mind. Examples: plants, animals, bodies.
Its existence depends on matter both externally and mentally.
3
Understand the second: mathematical science
Mathematical objects exist in matter externally but can be abstracted in the mind (e.g., numbers, shapes).
Existence attached to matter externally but not mentally.
4
Understand the third: divine science
Divine science deals with objects that do not depend on matter at all (e.g., God, angels).
Not attached to matter either externally or mentally.
5
Note the impossibility of a fourth
The author asserts there is no science whose subject exists only in the mind without external existence – that would be imaginary.
The fourth possibility (attached mentally but not externally) is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستراب في أن الغرض من الحكمة أن يوقف على حقائق الأشياء بحسب ما يمكنه الوقوف عليه. فإن الاستعداد للسعادة كما هو حقه لا يحصل إلا بالاطلاع على الحقائق وهو الاطلاع على العلوم الحكمية بالحقيقة. وما يكون منها فذلك لا يمكن أن يتغير بتغير الأزمان ولا أن يتبدل بتبدل الأديان كذلك بل يكون كما كان من الأزل إلى الأبد. فإن علمنا بأن الكل أعظم من جزئه مثلا، لا يمكن أن يكون في الأزل على خلاف ما يكون في الأبد على أنه من حيث هو هو ينقسم إلى أقسام مختلفة، فإن منها ما يكون مطلوبًا لا لذاته بل للغير وذلك أن يكون معونة لتحصيل ما عداه من العلوم وهو المنطق، ومنها ما يكون مطلوبًا لذاته.
وذلك إما أن يكون علمًا بما لا يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم النظرية، وإما أن يكون علما بما يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم العملية؛ والعلوم العملية قد تسمى فلسفة عمليةً، والمقصود من النظرية اعتقاد رأي فقط، ومن العملية اعتقاد رأي لأجل العمل، فالنظرية أولى بأن تُنسب إلى الرأي وما يكون فيها من الموجودات.
فذلك قد يكون من جملة ما يخالط الحركة نحو الإنسانية مثلا، وقد يكون من جملة ما لا يخالط نحو الملكية. والأول إما أن لا يمكن تجرده عن مادة معينة لا في القوام ولا في الوهم، وإما أن يمكن وذلك في الوهم لا في القوام. وبالجملة فالعلوم النظرية تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام؛ لأنه [أ] إما أن يكون وجوده متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج والذهن جميعا وهو العلم الطبيعي، [ب] أو متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج لا في الذهن وهو الرياضي [ت] أو لا يكون متعلقا بالمادة لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن وهو الإلهي؛ ولا مجال للقسم الرابع فإن من المحال أن يكون متعلقا بالمادة في الذهن لا في الخارج.
ثم كل واحد من الطبيعي والرياضي والإلهي فإنه قد يقوم مقام الأصل؛ وذلك على أقسام، وقد يقوم مقام الفرع؛ وذلك على أقسام كذلك يعرف من الكتب المخصوصة ببيانها.
وأما العلوم العملية فإنها تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام أيضًا؛ وذلك لأن ما يكون منها فذلك [أ] إما أن يكون متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المخصوصة بالإنسان وحده من الفضائل والرذائل؛ وهو علم الأخلاق [ب] أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين خواصه؛ وهو علم تدبير المنزل، [ت] أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين عامة الناس؛ وهو علم تدبير المدينة فهذه هي العلوم السياسية: الأول للنفس، والثاني لأهل المنزل، والثالث لأهل المدينة؛ على أن المشهور من علم السياسة والمفهوم منه هو الثالث منها.
ثم الغاية في الحكمة النظرية كما مرَّ ذكرها من قبل - معرفة الحق، وفي العملية معرفة الخير. ومن يؤتى الاستكمال بها من الحكمتين فقد أوتي خيرًا كثيرًا؛ وَذَلِكَ فَضْلُ اللَّهِ يُؤْتِيهِ مَن يَشَاءُ وَاللَّهُ ذُو الْفَضْلِ الْعَظِيمِ.
There is no doubt that the purpose of wisdom is to arrive at the realities of things as far as possible. For readiness for happiness, as is its due, is only attained by insight into realities, which is insight into the philosophical sciences in truth. And what pertains to them cannot change with changing times nor alter with changing religions, but remains as it was from eternity to eternity. For our knowledge that the whole is greater than its part, for example, cannot be in eternity contrary to what it is in eternity. Since it in itself divides into different divisions, among them is that which is sought not for itself but for something else, being an aid to acquiring other sciences—that is logic—and among them is that which is sought for itself. And that is either knowledge of what does not exist by our choice, which is theoretical science, or knowledge of what exists by our choice, which is practical science. Practical sciences may be called practical philosophy. The aim of theoretical is mere belief in an opinion, and of practical is belief in an opinion for the sake of action. Thus theoretical is more properly attributed to opinion and to what exists among existents. That may be part of what is mixed with motion, like humanity, for example, or part of what is not mixed, like divinity. The first is either incapable of being abstracted from a specific matter both in substance and in estimation, or capable of abstraction in estimation but not in substance. In summary, theoretical sciences divide into three divisions: [A] either its existence is attached to matter both externally and mentally, which is natural science; [B] or attached to matter externally but not mentally, which is mathematical; [T] or not attached to matter either externally or mentally, which is divine. There is no fourth division, for it is impossible that it be attached to matter mentally but not externally. Then each of natural, mathematical, and divine may serve as the original, which has its own divisions, or as the derivative, which likewise has its own divisions, known from the books specifically devoted to their explanation. As for practical sciences, they also divide into three divisions: [A] either attached to opinions that regulate the states specific to the individual alone, of virtues and vices—that is ethics; [B] or attached to opinions that regulate the states common between him and his household—that is household management; [T] or attached to opinions that regulate the states common between him and the general public—that is political science. These are the political sciences: the first for the soul, the second for the household, the third for the city, though the commonly known political science is the third. Then the goal of theoretical wisdom, as mentioned before, is knowledge of truth, and of practical wisdom is knowledge of good. Whoever attains perfection through these two wisdoms has attained abundant good. And that is the bounty of Allah which He gives to whom He wills, and Allah is the possessor of great bounty.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of sciences. It begins by stating the purpose of wisdom: to know realities as far as possible, which is necessary for happiness. The author then divides sciences into two categories: those sought as means (instrumental) and those sought for themselves. Logic is instrumental. The sought-for-themselves sciences are further divided into theoretical and practical based on whether their subject matter depends on our choice. Theoretical sciences aim at belief alone; practical sciences aim at action. The theoretical sciences are subdivided into three: natural science (deals with material things that cannot be abstracted from matter), mathematical science (deals with material things that can be abstracted mentally), and divine science (deals with immaterial things). The author asserts there is no fourth type. Then he notes that each of these can be either original or derivative. Practical sciences are also three: ethics (individual character), household management (family affairs), and politics (city affairs). The passage ends by stating the ultimate aims: truth for theoretical wisdom, good for practical wisdom, and praises Allah. In Islamic tradition, these correspond to known disciplines: natural science studies physical bodies (e.g., plants, animals), mathematics studies numbers and shapes, divine science studies God and angels. Practical: ethics deals with virtues like justice, household management with family relations, politics with just governance.

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Three Practical Sciences: Ethics, Household Management, Politics
Author subdivides practical sciences into ethics, household management, and politics based on scope of regulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three practical sciences (ethics, household management, politics) and explain their respective scopes of regulation.

Core Idea
Practical wisdom governs human action at three nested levels: the individual, the household, and the city.

Having covered theoretical sciences, the author turns to practical sciences. These are concerned with actions that we can choose. The three types are organized by the scope of human relations: the self, the family, and the larger society. This mirrors the classical division of practical philosophy in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the division of practical sciences
The passage lists three practical sciences: ethics (al-akhlāq), household management (tadbīr al-manzil), and politics (tadbīr al-madīna). Find the Arabic markers أ, ب, ت.
These are also called 'political sciences' in a broad sense.
2
Understand the first: ethics
Ethics deals with opinions that regulate the states specific to the individual alone, such as virtues and vices.
Focus on the individual's character.
3
Understand the second: household management
Household management deals with opinions that regulate the states common between a person and his household (family and dependents).
Includes relations with spouse, children, servants.
4
Understand the third: politics
Politics deals with opinions that regulate the states common between a person and the general public; it is the commonly understood political science.
Governs the city or state.
5
Note the collective term 'political sciences'
All three are called 'political sciences' because they concern community at different levels: first for the soul, second for the household, third for the city.
The third is the most famous as political science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستراب في أن الغرض من الحكمة أن يوقف على حقائق الأشياء بحسب ما يمكنه الوقوف عليه. فإن الاستعداد للسعادة كما هو حقه لا يحصل إلا بالاطلاع على الحقائق وهو الاطلاع على العلوم الحكمية بالحقيقة. وما يكون منها فذلك لا يمكن أن يتغير بتغير الأزمان ولا أن يتبدل بتبدل الأديان كذلك بل يكون كما كان من الأزل إلى الأبد. فإن علمنا بأن الكل أعظم من جزئه مثلا، لا يمكن أن يكون في الأزل على خلاف ما يكون في الأبد على أنه من حيث هو هو ينقسم إلى أقسام مختلفة، فإن منها ما يكون مطلوبًا لا لذاته بل للغير وذلك أن يكون معونة لتحصيل ما عداه من العلوم وهو المنطق، ومنها ما يكون مطلوبًا لذاته.
وذلك إما أن يكون علمًا بما لا يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم النظرية، وإما أن يكون علما بما يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم العملية؛ والعلوم العملية قد تسمى فلسفة عمليةً، والمقصود من النظرية اعتقاد رأي فقط، ومن العملية اعتقاد رأي لأجل العمل، فالنظرية أولى بأن تُنسب إلى الرأي وما يكون فيها من الموجودات.
فذلك قد يكون من جملة ما يخالط الحركة نحو الإنسانية مثلا، وقد يكون من جملة ما لا يخالط نحو الملكية. والأول إما أن لا يمكن تجرده عن مادة معينة لا في القوام ولا في الوهم، وإما أن يمكن وذلك في الوهم لا في القوام. وبالجملة فالعلوم النظرية تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام؛ لأنه [أ] إما أن يكون وجوده متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج والذهن جميعا وهو العلم الطبيعي، [ب] أو متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج لا في الذهن وهو الرياضي [ت] أو لا يكون متعلقا بالمادة لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن وهو الإلهي؛ ولا مجال للقسم الرابع فإن من المحال أن يكون متعلقا بالمادة في الذهن لا في الخارج.
ثم كل واحد من الطبيعي والرياضي والإلهي فإنه قد يقوم مقام الأصل؛ وذلك على أقسام، وقد يقوم مقام الفرع؛ وذلك على أقسام كذلك يعرف من الكتب المخصوصة ببيانها.
وأما العلوم العملية فإنها تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام أيضًا؛ وذلك لأن ما يكون منها فذلك [أ] إما أن يكون متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المخصوصة بالإنسان وحده من الفضائل والرذائل؛ وهو علم الأخلاق [ب] أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين خواصه؛ وهو علم تدبير المنزل، [ت] أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين عامة الناس؛ وهو علم تدبير المدينة فهذه هي العلوم السياسية: الأول للنفس، والثاني لأهل المنزل، والثالث لأهل المدينة؛ على أن المشهور من علم السياسة والمفهوم منه هو الثالث منها.
ثم الغاية في الحكمة النظرية كما مرَّ ذكرها من قبل - معرفة الحق، وفي العملية معرفة الخير. ومن يؤتى الاستكمال بها من الحكمتين فقد أوتي خيرًا كثيرًا؛ وَذَلِكَ فَضْلُ اللَّهِ يُؤْتِيهِ مَن يَشَاءُ وَاللَّهُ ذُو الْفَضْلِ الْعَظِيمِ.
There is no doubt that the purpose of wisdom is to arrive at the realities of things as far as possible. For readiness for happiness, as is its due, is only attained by insight into realities, which is insight into the philosophical sciences in truth. And what pertains to them cannot change with changing times nor alter with changing religions, but remains as it was from eternity to eternity. For our knowledge that the whole is greater than its part, for example, cannot be in eternity contrary to what it is in eternity. Since it in itself divides into different divisions, among them is that which is sought not for itself but for something else, being an aid to acquiring other sciences—that is logic—and among them is that which is sought for itself. And that is either knowledge of what does not exist by our choice, which is theoretical science, or knowledge of what exists by our choice, which is practical science. Practical sciences may be called practical philosophy. The aim of theoretical is mere belief in an opinion, and of practical is belief in an opinion for the sake of action. Thus theoretical is more properly attributed to opinion and to what exists among existents. That may be part of what is mixed with motion, like humanity, for example, or part of what is not mixed, like divinity. The first is either incapable of being abstracted from a specific matter both in substance and in estimation, or capable of abstraction in estimation but not in substance. In summary, theoretical sciences divide into three divisions: [A] either its existence is attached to matter both externally and mentally, which is natural science; [B] or attached to matter externally but not mentally, which is mathematical; [T] or not attached to matter either externally or mentally, which is divine. There is no fourth division, for it is impossible that it be attached to matter mentally but not externally. Then each of natural, mathematical, and divine may serve as the original, which has its own divisions, or as the derivative, which likewise has its own divisions, known from the books specifically devoted to their explanation. As for practical sciences, they also divide into three divisions: [A] either attached to opinions that regulate the states specific to the individual alone, of virtues and vices—that is ethics; [B] or attached to opinions that regulate the states common between him and his household—that is household management; [T] or attached to opinions that regulate the states common between him and the general public—that is political science. These are the political sciences: the first for the soul, the second for the household, the third for the city, though the commonly known political science is the third. Then the goal of theoretical wisdom, as mentioned before, is knowledge of truth, and of practical wisdom is knowledge of good. Whoever attains perfection through these two wisdoms has attained abundant good. And that is the bounty of Allah which He gives to whom He wills, and Allah is the possessor of great bounty.

The author then divides sciences into two categories: those sought as means (instrumental) and those sought for themselves. Logic is instrumental. Theoretical sciences aim at belief alone; practical sciences aim at action. Is Logic a Science? A Verbal Dispute
Author analyzes the verbal dispute over logic being a science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a disagreement is a verbal dispute (bahth lafzi) by analyzing definitions of key terms.

Core Idea
Whether logic is a science depends entirely on your definition of 'science'.

This passage examines whether logic qualifies as a science. The author argues that the disagreement is merely verbal: it hinges on how 'science' is defined. If science requires studying external existence, logic (which studies mental concepts) is not a science; if it includes all existence, logic is a science. Recognizing such verbal disputes helps us avoid false disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of 'science'
Find in the Arabic where the author presents two contrasting conditions for something to be a science: one that restricts science to external existence, and one that includes both mental and external existence.
Look for the phrases 'عما به وجود في الخارج' and 'البحث عن كل ما له وجود سواءً كان في الذهن أو في الخارج'.
2
Apply each definition to logic
Logic deals with mental concepts (secondary intelligibles), not external objects. According to the first definition, logic is not a science; according to the second, it is. See how the same subject changes classification.
3
Recognize the verbal nature of the dispute
The disagreement disappears once the definition is clarified. The author calls this 'khalaf lafzi' – a dispute over words, not substance. Verify that the opposing scholars would agree if they used the same definition.
The Arabic terms 'اختلاف لفظي' are explicitly used.
4
Apply to other Islamic sciences
Think of a similar debate in usul al-fiqh: whether qiyas (analogy) is a source of law. If 'source' means independent proof, qiyas is not a source; if it means a method to derive rulings, it is. This pattern mirrors the logic debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الاختلاف بأنه من العلوم أو لا اختلاف لفظي؛ إذ البحث عما به وجود في الخارج إن كان معتبراً في العلم، فالمنطق ليس بعلم. وإن لم يكن معتبراً فيه ذلك بل المعتبر هو البحث عن كل ما له وجود سواءً كان في الذهن أو في الخارج فإنه علم. وقد نقل عن الشيخ أنه قال إنه رئيس العلوم وعن الفارابي إنه خادم العلوم، وهذا بحث لفظي أيضًا. فإن المفهوم من «الرئيس» إذا كان مشروطًا بشرط أن يكون مقصودًا بنفسه؛ والمنطق ليس كذلك؛ إذ هو مقصود لغيره فلا يكون رئيسا . وإن لم يكن مشروطًا بذلك الشرط، بل الرئيس هو الذي يحتاج إليه غيره من غير عكس، والمنطق كذلك فهو رئيس. وعلى هذا كونه «خادما»؛ إذ البحث فيه بحث لفظي أيضًا؛ فنبحث عن مفهومه بأنه ما هو وننظر فيه أنه هل يوجد في نفس المنطق ذلك أم لا يوجد، ولا يبعد أن يكون خادمًا ورئيسًا. وقد قيل: سيد القوم خادمهم، والسيد هو الرئيس.
Then the disagreement about whether it [logic] is one of the sciences or not is a verbal dispute. For if the consideration in science is about what has existence in the external world, then logic is not a science. But if that is not considered, rather the consideration is the investigation of everything that has existence, whether in the mind or in the external world, then it is a science. And it is narrated from the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] that he said it is the chief of sciences, and from al-Farabi that it is the servant of sciences. This is also a verbal dispute. For the concept of 'chief', if it is conditioned by being intended for itself, then logic is not like that because it is intended for something else, so it is not chief. But if it is not conditioned by that condition, rather the chief is that which others need without the reverse, and logic is like that, then it is chief. And likewise for its being 'servant'. For the inquiry about its concept is also a verbal dispute. So we investigate its concept: what is it? And we consider whether that exists in logic itself or not. And it is not far that it be both servant and chief. And it has been said: 'The leader of a people is their servant', and the leader is the chief.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a classification analysis. The author begins by stating that the debate over whether logic is a science ('ilm) is a verbal dispute (khalaf lafzi). If science is defined as the study of external existence, then logic (which deals with mental concepts) is not a science. But if science includes the study of all existence (mental and external), then logic qualifies. Next, the author addresses the famous claims that logic is 'chief of sciences' (from Ibn Sina) and 'servant of sciences' (from al-Farabi). He shows this is also a verbal dispute: if 'chief' means something pursued for its own sake, logic is not chief; but if it means something that others need without needing them (as a tool), logic is chief. Similarly, 'servant' can be understood such that logic can be both servant and chief. The author concludes with a saying: 'The leader of a people is their servant,' indicating that the roles are not mutually exclusive. This analysis helps the student understand that such classifications are not absolute but depend on definitions. An example from usul al-fiqh: Usul al-fiqh is a tool for deriving rulings; it can be considered 'chief' because other sciences depend on it, and 'servant' because it serves the science of fiqh.

Key Terms
علم
بحث لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the source texts are contradictory.

Key Takeaway
A disagreement is verbal when it disappears upon clarifying the definition of the key term involved.

Logic as Chief or Servant: Another Verbal Dispute
Author analyzes the verbal dispute over logic being chief or servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the same verbal dispute analysis to the classification of logic as chief or servant of sciences.

Core Idea
Logic can be both chief and servant depending on how you define those roles.

Ibn Sina called logic the 'chief of sciences', while al-Farabi called it the 'servant of sciences'. Are they contradicting each other? The author shows this is another verbal dispute. If 'chief' means something pursued for its own sake, logic is not chief; but if it means something other sciences depend on, logic is chief. Similarly, 'servant' can be understood in a way that makes logic both servant and chief. This teaches us that titles like these are not absolute but depend on perspective.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two contrasting claims
Identify the two reported sayings: Ibn Sina's 'raid al-ulum' (chief of sciences) and al-Farabi's 'khadim al-ulum' (servant of sciences). Note that the author presents them as part of a verbal dispute.
The Arabic phrases are 'رئيس العلوم' and 'خادم العلوم'.
2
Analyze the definition of 'chief'
The author gives two possible definitions of 'chief': (1) something intended for itself (maqsud bi dhatihi), and (2) something that others need but does not need them. Apply each to logic: logic is not intended for itself (it serves other sciences), so by the first definition it is not chief; but by the second, it is chief because other sciences depend on it.
Look for the conditions 'مشروطًا بشرط أن يكون مقصودًا بنفسه' and 'الرئيس هو الذي يحتاج إليه غيره من غير عكس'.
3
Analyze the definition of 'servant'
Similarly, 'servant' can be defined in a way that is compatible with being chief. The author cites a saying: 'The leader of a people is their servant', indicating that chief and servant are not mutually exclusive.
The saying is 'سيد القوم خادمهم'.
4
Apply to other Islamic sciences
Consider usul al-fiqh. It is a servant because it provides principles for fiqh, but it is also chief because fiqh depends on it. Identify other sciences (e.g., hadith studies) that have a similar dual role.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition can logic be considered 'chief of sciences'?
A) If 'chief' means something intended for itself.
B) If 'chief' means something that others need but does not need them.
C) If 'chief' means it is the most difficult science.
D) If 'chief' means it is independent of other sciences.

Key Takeaway
Titles like 'chief' and 'servant' are relative and depend on definition; a discipline can hold both roles simultaneously.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Why the Logician Must Study Words
Author argues for the necessity of studying words in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument for the necessity of studying words in logic and distinguish between words as a necessary tool and the true subject of logic (meanings).

Core Idea
Words are the necessary vehicle for logic, not its destination.

This passage addresses a fundamental question: why does a logician, who deals with meanings and concepts, need to study words? The author argues that necessity arises from two constraints: we must communicate with others, and our own minds cannot order thoughts without linguistic representation. This lesson walks through that argument and clarifies that words are a means, not the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hypothetical scenario
The author begins by considering a world where words are unnecessary for logic: if one could study logic purely mentally and convey thoughts directly without words. This sets up the argument by showing what would make words dispensable.
2
Recognize two impossibilities
The author then states that such a scenario is impossible because (a) we must engage in address or dialogue (communication), and (b) the human mind cannot arrange meanings without imagining corresponding words. Understand why these are considered insurmountable constraints.
3
Extract the consequence for logic
Since words are always present in thought and communication, they have different states (e.g., ambiguity, specificity) that affect the meanings they convey. Hence, the logician must consider those linguistic states, because meanings acquire properties from words that they would not have otherwise.
The author concludes: 'Thus words—rather, their states—are necessarily considered in the art of logic.'
4
Note the universal scope
The author emphasizes that this necessity is not tied to any particular language: the purpose is to access meanings, which are universal. Therefore, logical analysis of words applies across all languages, though the examples may come from Arabic.
5
Distinguish from a common misconception
The author rejects the view that the subject of logic is words themselves. Instead, words are a necessary but secondary consideration. The true subject is meanings (ma'ani), but words must still be examined due to the constraints above.
This clarifies that logic is not mere linguistics; it uses linguistic analysis as a tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النظر إلى الألفاظ فإنه من جملة ما تدعو إليه الضرورة؛ إذ المنطقي لا يشتغل بها إلا من جملة المخاطبة أو المحاورة. ولو أمكنه أن يطلع على صناعة المنطق بفكره الساذج دون الاستعانة بتعلّم مخاطبة أو محاورة، وأمكنه أن يطلع المحاور فيه على ما في ذهنه من غير أن يتعرض للألفاظ، فلا حاجة له إلى اللفظ بلفظ ما البتة. لكن لما كانت الضرورة تدعو إلى استعمال الألفاظ وقد كان من المتعذر أيضًا على الرّويّة أن ترتيب المعاني من غير أن يتخيل معها الألفاظ الدالة عليها، بل كانت الرّويّة بينة في الذهن بألفاظ متخيلة مطابقة لزم أن يكون لألفاظ أحوال مختلفة تختلف لأجلها أحوال ما يطابقها في الذهن من المعاني، حتى تصير لها أحكام لولا الألفاظ لم تكن. فتكون الألفاظ بل أحوالها منظورة إليها في صناعة المنطق لا محالة. وحينئذ تكون الألفاظ محتاجة إليها، وتلك الألفاظ لا تكون مختصة بلغة قوم دون قوم أصلا؛ لما أن المقصود منها هو الاطلاع على المعاني وذلك من جملة ما لا اختصاص له بقوم على ما عرّف. ولما تعرفت على حقيقة الموضوع وعلى ما يتعلق به من الموجودات والجهات التي بها هي الموضوع، فلا تلتفت إلى قول من يقول: إن موضوع المنطق هو النظر في الألفاظ من حيث إنها تدل على المعاني، وإن المنطقي صناعته أن يتكلم على الألفاظ من حيث إنها تدل على معانيها بالمطابقة أو بغيرها»، فإن ذلك من قبيل ما لا يلتفت وهذا ظاهر.
As for consideration of words, it is among the things to which necessity calls; for the logician does not engage in them except as part of address or dialogue. If it were possible for him to become acquainted with the art of logic by his pure thought without the aid of learning address or dialogue, and if it were possible for him to inform the interlocutor of what is in his mind without exposing words, then he would have no need for words at all. But since necessity calls for the use of words, and it was also impossible for the human mind to arrange meanings without imagining with them the words that indicate them—rather, the mind's notions are in the intellect with corresponding imagined words—it necessarily follows that words have different states, on account of which the states of the meanings that correspond to them in the intellect differ, so that they acquire properties that without words they would not have. Thus words—rather, their states—are necessarily considered in the art of logic. Then words are in need of that [consideration], and those words are not specific to the language of any people over others at all, because the intended purpose is the understanding of meanings, and that is among things that are not specific to any people, as was defined. And since you have learned the true nature of the subject and what pertains to it of existents and the respects by which it is the subject, do not pay attention to the saying of one who says: 'The subject of logic is the consideration of words insofar as they indicate meanings, and the logician's craft is to speak about words insofar as they indicate their meanings by conformity or otherwise'—for that is among the things not to be heeded, and this is clear.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is an argument for why the study of words (alfaz) is necessary in logic, even though the subject of logic is meanings (ma'ani). The author begins by stating that necessity compels the logician to consider words because he must communicate or dialogue. If it were possible to study logic purely mentally and convey thoughts without words, there would be no need for them. However, such is impossible: the mind cannot arrange meanings without imagining corresponding words, and meanings in the mind are always accompanied by mental images of words. Consequently, words have different states (ahwal) that affect the states of the meanings they correspond to, leading to properties that meanings would not have without words. Therefore, words and their states must be considered in the art of logic. The author then emphasizes that words are not specific to any particular language because the goal is to access meanings, which are universal. He concludes by rejecting the view that the subject of logic is words themselves insofar as they indicate meanings—such a view is not worth heeding. The argument flows from a hypothetical impossibility to a necessary inclusion, grounding the role of linguistic analysis in logic without making it the primary subject.

المعاني
المخاطبة أو المحاورة

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it necessary for the logician to study words?
A) Because words are the subject matter of logic.
B) Because the mind cannot arrange meanings without imagining words, and communication requires them.
C) Because each language has unique logical rules.
D) Because words are more important than meanings.

Key Takeaway
Words are an integral part of logic because they are the medium of thought and communication, but they are not the subject itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following scenario: A student of logic says, 'I don't need to study Arabic grammar because logic is about universal meanings, not about particular languages.' Using the author's argument from this passage, explain why that student's position is flawed. Your explanation should reference the two impossibilities (communication and mental representation) and the conclusion that words must be studied.
?
Identify a passage from the Quran or hadith where the meaning changes based on a linguistic nuance (e.g., the word 'قَرْء' in the verse on divorce, which can mean both menstrual period and purity). Explain how the logician's awareness of word states (ahwal) is necessary to correctly understand the ruling.
